<div id="review_612138579" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/612138579" />
    <a title="Kinga" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/110812-kinga"><img alt="Kinga" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491308194p2/110812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/612138579?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kinga" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kinga" href="/user/show/110812-kinga">Kinga</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/110812-kinga?shelf=pub-2012">pub-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer612138579" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4030103051267298521">My sister and I are like twins born four years apart. She is my best friend and I would give her both of my kidneys. Yet, it hasn't always been this way. There was a time that even though we still felt obliged to love each other, we found we had little in common. Four years can be a serious obstacle when you're a teenager. We also belonged to an entirely different social groups. My sister was more of a cheap wine, flannel shirts, suicidal rock singers kind of girl while I tended to find my solac</span>
  <span id="freeText4030103051267298521" style="display:none">My sister and I are like twins born four years apart. She is my best friend and I would give her both of my kidneys. Yet, it hasn't always been this way. There was a time that even though we still felt obliged to love each other, we found we had little in common. Four years can be a serious obstacle when you're a teenager. We also belonged to an entirely different social groups. My sister was more of a cheap wine, flannel shirts, suicidal rock singers kind of girl while I tended to find my solace in weed, hoop earrings and rappers who'd been shot dead.<br /><br />We didn't see eye to eye. Sometimes we wouldn't even acknowledge each other on the street.<br /><br />Now, it's all different and literature is one of those things that keeps bringing us closer and closer. We read this book together on our respective Kindles and decided to review it in a recorded conversation, here translated from Polish for your benefit:<br /><br /><b>Kinga:</b> I decided that 'The Dog Stars' is a post-apocalyptic novel about fishing. How did you like it?<br /><br /><b>Basia:</b> I have mixed feelings. I did like the message and concept but I believe that the author didn't use their full potential, he focused too much on fishing and flying and old Cessna and at times it wasn't even so much a post-apocalyptic novel about fishing, but a self-help guide titled: "The World Ended - What To Do Now?"<br /><br /><b>Kinga:</b> Personally, I do like books about fishing, roaming the forests and some general unhurried meditating, although you might be right that for the first 80 pages the narrator was ordered to wander about while the author was just starting to figure out the plot.<br /><br /><b>Basia:</b> My feelings exactly. It was as if the author created this post-apocalyptic world (a very believable, it has to be said), and during writing he remembered a novel should also lead somewhere, that there should be a climax, so while the main character is flying his Cessna (playing for time), the author comes up with what happens next.<br />But let's start from the beginning: we are in the United States, the majority of population died of flu. The narrator who guides us around this post-apocalyptic world is Hig, who is somewhat forced to share his new life with Bangley. The creation of these two main characters was one the things I really liked about this book, although I think some people might find these two too stereotypical.<br /><br /><b>Kinga:</b> I really liked these characters too. And I didn't find them particularly stereotypical. In the beginning I thought Hig was a really tough guy, because he can swear and shoot a deer, but then when see him through Bangley's eyes, we notice his weakness (or, if you like, his humanity) and we see that Bangley is taking care of Big Hig, at the same time revealing his own weakness. In reality, 'The Dog Stars' is a bit of a Western. Two guys sit in some God-forgotten hole, they talk little, shoot a lot, sometimes they would feed a corpse to the dog, but in all of it they grew to love each other. And as it is in all Westerns there comes a woman, traditionally devoid of personality, and her main feature are her violet eyes. That woman is a nurturer, care-taker and all-round chicken soup for the soul.<br /><br /><b>Basia:</b> You thought of Westerns and I thought of two philosophers who spoke of anarchy and the creating of nation. And there is no better place and time to test such theories than right after an apocalypse, where everything that the humankind had built falls apart and it can finally be established whether the natural state for humans is to be happy and peaceful like Locke had it (here Hig) or whether it's a state of constant struggle brought forward by the innate selfishness of people like Hobbes maintained (starring Bangley).<br />So what I think Peter Heller is doing here is that he is proposing a thought experiment of a sort: imagine that all the humandkind expired and there are only two dudes left with completely opposite outlooks on the world. What will happen and will those outlooks even matter anymore?<br /><br /><br /><b>Kinga:</b> I also thought about this dichotomy. And that even in those almost lab-like environment such as that post-apocalyptic world it was very difficult to test these theories. Because on one hand we have Bangley, who preventively shoots everyone and on the other hand we have Hig who is free to test his theory of loving one's neighbour but he will only be able to be wrong once. <br /><br />To be perfectly honest I thought that it would've looked different in Europe due to purely technical reasons - it would be next to impossible to gather such an arsenal like in the US, and it would be somewhat harder for people to kill each other, so they would be forced to talk to each other. And once they start talking, it might turn out it's better to be together than against each other. I think this was the final optimistic conclusion of this novel.<br /><br /><b>Basia:</b> I believe that paradoxically this novel was supposed to be uplifting after all and it wanted to remind us something important. From the flashbacks we can gather that the characters' lives before the catastrophe were full of waiting for something, until they make enough money, can start a family, waiting for the courage to change their careers. Waiting for that real life to begin. And of course only with the hindsight and with the apocalypse in mind can they realise that that was the real life, those were the best moments.<br /><br /><i>Funny how you can live a whole life waiting and not know it. [...] Waiting for your real life to begin. Maybe the most real thing the end.</i><br /><br />Maybe those quotes, away from the novel, might sound trite and gushing but amongst that jagged and austere narrative made quite of an impression on me.<br /><br /><b>Kinga:</b> I agree. I think in general this is the appeal of post-apocalyptic novels. All those things, that we fret over, even though we realise they're not that important, get cancelled and life returns to the basics. In real life, only selected few have it in them to just reject all that nonsense, the rest console themselves with reading. As a matter of fact, most books about fishing are not really about fishing but also about what's important in life. And it turns out that what's important it's to find someone we can love and everything will somehow works itself out. Even if that someone is just a dog.<br /><br />You mentioned the jagged, austere style. I liked it a lot. It was as if that whole trauma of the apocalypse deprived people of the ability to talk. Loneliness and sadness drive Hig to speak like a troglodyte, there are many things left unsaid and sentences end with prepositions that should be followed by a noun, but Hig can't bring himself to say it. There was a great scene of a conversation between Hig and Bangley, which I'll quote here:<br /><br /><i>I asked him if he ever thought there was anything more than this, than just surviving day to day. Recon, fixing the plane, growing the five vegetables, trapping a rabbit. Like what are we waiting for? His chair, crick crick, stopped. He got very still like a hunter that smelled an animal on the wind. Close. Like he woke up.<br />Say again.<br />More than this. Day to day.<br />He worked his jaw. His mineral eyes graying in the fading light. Like maybe I’d tipped over the edge. Gotta go he said. Stood up.</i><br /><br /><br /><b>Basia:</b> This is exactly how I felt about the style. I was enchanted by the language, especially after my trials and tribulations with Doris Lessing and her 'Memoirs of a Survivor', where, by contrast, the narrative is formed by long, fancy sentences always ending with an ellipsis. Actually the only thing I didn't quite like about this novel was that despite great world-building and a fantastic style, simply not enough happened. On the other hand, now I'm thinking... what can happen when the world ended?<br /><br /><b>Kinga:</b> I feel the same way - we expect such novels to be action-packed but really it's all after the. After the.<br /><br /><br />PS. If you would like to see some of our childhood photos which accompany this review on my blog, click here <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://kinga-thebooksnob.blogspot.co.uk/2013/06/peter-heller-dog-stars.html">http://kinga-thebooksnob.blogspot.co....</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4030103051267298521" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JanB " class="user" itemprop="url" name="JanB " href="/user/show/260116-janb">JanB </a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3094122330" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18127809407281153399">3.5 stars <br/>Hig has survived the flu pandemic, although he’s lost his wife and everyone he holds dear. He now lives in an airport hangar with his dog Jasper and has an odd relationship/friendship with Bangley, who has survival skills as well as a cache of weapons. Bangley protects their perimeter from marauders and doesn’t hesitate to use his guns. He and Hig are as different as night and day but they need each other. <br/><br/>Hig lives mostly inside his head, out of necessity. He recalls memories of happi</span>
  <span id="freeText18127809407281153399" style="display:none">3.5 stars <br />Hig has survived the flu pandemic, although he’s lost his wife and everyone he holds dear. He now lives in an airport hangar with his dog Jasper and has an odd relationship/friendship with Bangley, who has survival skills as well as a cache of weapons. Bangley protects their perimeter from marauders and doesn’t hesitate to use his guns. He and Hig are as different as night and day but they need each other. <br /><br />Hig lives mostly inside his head, out of necessity. He recalls memories of happier days, and he deals with the harsh reality of his current circumstances. <br /><br />The writing style is one of no quotation marks and sentence fragments. This would drive me crazy if reading the print book but on audio it worked perfectly. <br /><br />A post-apocalyptic book that is grim but not without hope and beauty. I enjoyed this book but of the three Peter Heller books I recently read this was my least favorite. This says more about me than the book. And even thought it was my least favorite of the 3 I read I still enjoyed it very much.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18127809407281153399" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2946228681" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2946228681" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8051792-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544530609p2/8051792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2946228681?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/8051792-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8051792-lisa?shelf=2019-favorites">2019-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2946228681" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16600578335691032236">[4.5] <i>The Dog Stars</i> is about survival in the face of grief. About finding beauty in the midst of desolation. I was vacationing in Colorado while reading this novel and was awed by Heller’s way of writing about the landscape. I came to care for Hig and became invested in his relationships. (Oh and I loved Jasper!) The novel strikes a rare balance - between pulse pounding and mediative, disturbing and joyful. Stunning in every way!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_403446247" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/403446247" />
    <a title="Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius"><img alt="Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563066276p2/1085121.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/403446247?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*" href="/user/show/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius">Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780449013113" href="/book/show/13330553-the-dog-stars">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer403446247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8862834228421222402">If this book didn't have a dog as one of the mian characters this would have been a three star for me. <br/><br/>Hig survives a super flu out break that kills off everyone he loves except for his dog Jasper and his airplane The Beast (which is also the name of an awesome roller coaster at Kings Island in Cincinnatti). He teams up with a man who is now a sociopath, but might not have always been before the shit hit the fan. They hold up at a small airport that they can protect with the help of a tower, a f</span>
  <span id="freeText8862834228421222402" style="display:none">If this book didn't have a dog as one of the mian characters this would have been a three star for me. <br /><br />Hig survives a super flu out break that kills off everyone he loves except for his dog Jasper and his airplane The Beast (which is also the name of an awesome roller coaster at Kings Island in Cincinnatti). He teams up with a man who is now a sociopath, but might not have always been before the shit hit the fan. They hold up at a small airport that they can protect with the help of a tower, a few guns, good aim, a dog that growls at the sign of trouble and the Beast that Hig flys to scope the area for intruders. Because, you see, everyone turns mean and murderous.<br /><br />This is a problem I am beginning to have with books like this (I know it's distopian), the assumption when something really bad happens, like a super flu, everybody who had moral values prior to the event turn into killing machines automatically. I don't believe that is the way it would go down in reality. When 9/11 happened everyone pulled together and were kinder to one another than they were on 9/10, at least for a little while. So I believe if a pandemic were to sweep the globe and kill almost everyone, human nature would bring people together probably out of shear loneliness more than anything. "oh hi there! Good god it's been a long time since I've seen another face! Too bad we can't talk and get to know each other because I have to kill you.". Bang! "Now that's a shame."<br /><br />But that would'nt make for a very good story, and this story was about a bleak future for the human race. As far as that's concerned I think the author did a good job. I did have an issue with the part where after Hig finds a couple of other people and after he convinces them not to kill him and then plans to fly them out <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[, he does all this math to calculate how much weight he can safely carry on take off, figures out he would have to leave the man and take the woman...but barely. So the plan is to pick up the man from a near by stretch of highway where take off would be easier, ok, cool, that's smart. They load up the plane with gear, a couple of lambs, and the girlfriend and they just barely make it over the trees. Hey, dumbass, why didn't the girlfriend, gear and lambs go to the highway with the dad? I'm sure if the frail women couldn't walk some kind of cart pulled by left behind livestock could have been devised! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.......so that was kind of rediculus.<br /><br />But the best part of the book is the relationship between Hig and his dog Jasper. Jasper is all that is left of his old life, a reminder of how life used to be before. He did a good job with that.<br /><br />3.5<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8862834228421222402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_396150576" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/396150576" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4086118-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567611029p2/4086118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/396150576?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4086118-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780449013113" href="/book/show/13330553-the-dog-stars">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=aviation">aviation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=dog">dog</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=nature">nature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=colorado">colorado</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=global-warming">global-warming</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer396150576" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12278370201536255670">I loved this debut novel, a spare and bittersweet story of survival in rural Colorado after a disease induced apocalypse. The strength in this tale lies in Heller’s portrayal of grief stricken Hig, who is continually balancing his lyrical introspections on finding meaning in his narrowed life and his cherishing of the beauty that remains.<br/> <br/>Hig has been living a lonely life for nine years after an epidemic flu killed off most of humanity. He has settled at a remote airport north of Denver where th</span>
  <span id="freeText12278370201536255670" style="display:none">I loved this debut novel, a spare and bittersweet story of survival in rural Colorado after a disease induced apocalypse. The strength in this tale lies in Heller’s portrayal of grief stricken Hig, who is continually balancing his lyrical introspections on finding meaning in his narrowed life and his cherishing of the beauty that remains.<br> <br>Hig has been living a lonely life for nine years after an epidemic flu killed off most of humanity. He has settled at a remote airport north of Denver where there is solar power and fuel for his Cessna plane, houses nearby for scavenging, a plot for gardening, and open ground for his survivalist partner Bangley to efficiently kill off periodic marauders with his stockpiled arsenal. But Bangley doesn’t talk much, so Hig spends a lot of time talking to his blue heeler dog Jasper. Together they find some solace on hunting and fishing excursions, where he communes with nature and grieves over the loss of his wife and the loss of species due to global warming. His true escape comes from the sense of freedom he gets from flying. He scouts the perimeter for threats and periodically visits a community of Mennonites, from which he must keep his distance due to a blood disease slowly killing them off. But everything changes when he spots from his plane a remote box canyon with signs of civilized life: a house with smoke from a chimney and a woman out gardening.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380388347i/748810._SY540_.jpg" width="200" height="225" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380388347i/748811._SX540_.jpg" width="250" height="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Heller’s experience as a non-fiction “adventure writer” shows with his lovely telegraphic prose about nature, as in this piece about fishing with his dog:<br><i>Sometimes back then, fishing with Jasper up the Sulphur, I hit my limit. I mean it felt my heart might just burst. Bursting is different from breaking. Like there is no way to contain how beautiful. Not it either, not just beauty. Something about how I fit. This little bend of smooth stones, the leaning cliffs. The smell of spruce. The small cutthroat making quiet rings in the black water of a pool. And no need to thank even. Just be. Just fish. Just walk up the creek, get dark, get cold, it is all a piece. Of me somehow.</i><br><br>The relief he gets from his bird’s eye view of the world from his plane is wonderfully captured in this passage: <br><i>And for a time while flying, seeing all this as a hawk would see it, I am myself somehow freed from the sticky details: I am not grief sick nor stiffer in the joints nor ever lonely, nor someone who lives with the nausea of having killed and seems destined to kill again. I am the one who is flying over all of it looking down. Nothing can touch me.<br><br> There is no one to tell this to and yet it seems very important to get this right. The reality and what it is like to escape it. That even now it is too beautiful to bear.<br><br> Also I wonder how Bangley is built inside and everyone like him. He is at home in his solitude as the note reverberating inside a bell. Prefers it. Will protect it to the death. Lives for protecting it the way a peregrine lives for killing other birds midflight. Does not want to communicate what the death and the beauty do to each other inside him.</i><br><br>The stars in the book are a perpetual reminder of how small humans are in the scheme of things, as in this example:<br><i>Now we walk fast in the dark. Me and Jasper, the sled scraping behind. Cold. Good and cold. High stars nettle the black, no moon, crossing under the Milky Way like crossing a deep river. Never will get to the other side. We never do.</i><br><br>Like survivors of war, the killing it takes to exist can be taken in stride, but the associated losses along the way undermine the victory:<br><i>The dead goats multiply. You can pull a goat off into the field, but a memory you can only haul into the sun and hope it dessicates. Dries to something crumbled and odorless.</i><br><br>Hig is fond of certain Chinese poets and takes solace in these lines (which appear to be Heller’s own emulation):<br><i>We have traveled.<br>Now you will be the path<br>I will walk I will walk<br>Over you.</i><br><br>Thus, the book stands out from the “genre” of post-apocalyptic tales in its artful dwelling on what in life is worth preserving. The vignettes about nature and the love story in the novel suffer from being a bit too “precious”, but they moved me a lot nonetheless. Some other quotable quotes are collected <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/work/quotes/18538203-the-dog-stars" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.<br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="12278370201536255670" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_433220935" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/433220935" />
    <a title="Melki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5293536-melki"><img alt="Melki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308071306p2/5293536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/433220935?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melki" href="/user/show/5293536-melki">Melki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=up-at-the-crack-of-doom">up-at-the-crack-of-doom</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=dog-is-my-copilot">dog-is-my-copilot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer433220935" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6606966964341787761"><i>The flu killed almost everybody, then the blood disease killed more. The ones who are left are mostly Not Nice, why we live here on the plain, why I patrol every day.</i><br/><br/>I see that several reviewers are comparing this book to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road' title='The Road by Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Road</a>, so I'll jump on that bandwagon for a bit. While <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy' title='Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cormac McCarthy</a>'s book worried me, and gnawed at me, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13330761.The_Dog_Stars' title='The Dog Stars by Peter Heller' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Dog Stars</a> kept me at arm's length. I would have had no trouble putting this book down and not returning to it for a month. Or ever. I was so removed from the pligh</span>
  <span id="freeText6606966964341787761" style="display:none"><i>The flu killed almost everybody, then the blood disease killed more. The ones who are left are mostly Not Nice, why we live here on the plain, why I patrol every day.</i><br /><br />I see that several reviewers are comparing this book to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road" title="The Road by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Road</a>, so I'll jump on that bandwagon for a bit. While <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy" title="Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">Cormac McCarthy</a>'s book worried me, and gnawed at me, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13330761.The_Dog_Stars" title="The Dog Stars by Peter Heller" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dog Stars</a> kept me at arm's length. I would have had no trouble putting this book down and not returning to it for a month. Or ever. I was so removed from the plight of the character in this book - <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I didn't even cry when the dog died. And, that's NEVER happened before. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I have no real explanation for this disconnect. Perhaps it has to do with the main character's reason for living, and the fact that I couldn't really figure out what that was after (see spoiler). In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road" title="The Road by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Road</a>, the father must fight to save the child he loves, and love is a powerful and primal emotion. Hig, the protagonist of this book, loses everything he loves. His day to day existence is joyless and dismal. He seems to live to fly.<br /><br />So, to recap...this book pales in comparison with, well, you know - BUT - two saving graces: I did enjoy the relationship between Hig and his neighbor, Bangley. Here are two men with NOTHING in common, yet they have come to rely on one another for survival, and a strange friendship has evolved. I was also touched by Hig's concern for some Mennonite settlements ravaged by the blood disease. The fact that he hasn't completely given up on humanity serves him well, and leads to an unexpected conclusion to the book.<br /><br />Who knows? Judging from the number of five-star reviews, you may find plenty to love about this book. And, I certainly didn't hate it. I just wish I hadn't bought it in hardback.<br /><br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="6606966964341787761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1468412635" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1468412635" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45632570-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438789125p2/45632570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1468412635?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/45632570-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45632570-tim?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1468412635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17475251112387599176">This is like a Walt Disney version of The Road. <br/><br/>First of all, I’m going to nominate this for the worst sex writing award - “All those pieces. She moved. Her moving over me called them called them. The way a thousand fish rock together with the swell. Back and forth. The way the stars in the leaves. I reached. In her, in the very center, somewhere the single only stillness where everything cohered. Nothing but reach.”<br/><br/>It’s taken me a month to get through this relatively short book. Mainly this was</span>
  <span id="freeText17475251112387599176" style="display:none">This is like a Walt Disney version of The Road. <br /><br />First of all, I’m going to nominate this for the worst sex writing award - “All those pieces. She moved. Her moving over me called them called them. The way a thousand fish rock together with the swell. Back and forth. The way the stars in the leaves. I reached. In her, in the very center, somewhere the single only stillness where everything cohered. Nothing but reach.”<br /><br />It’s taken me a month to get through this relatively short book. Mainly this was down to the often absurdly pretentious nature of the prose. The world is all broken into serrated untidy fragments so Heller does the same to his prose. “"For the dog he said. Angry. Because I didn't do my job. To him." That kind of thing. Which soon begins to grate and become nothing but a backfiring and rather adolescent gimmick. Shame because there are lots of good things in this novel. My other problem was with Hig himself. You know sometimes when you just know the author is fictionalising himself for the main character and as a result is constantly pushing you as the reader to like him? Hig is like a man advertising himself on a dating site. He’s straining so hard to make himself seem attractive that you begin to dislike him. He does this by offsetting himself with his companion Bangley, a brutish taciturn redneck, born killer who fights off all intruders. Hig has scruples about killing. He loves fishing and hunting except for the part where he has to end a life. Because Hig, we’re relentlessly reminded, is a sensitive guy. It began to feel like Heller was imagining Hig from the comfort of an armchair, not immersed in a world where every stranger is a deranged serial killer. Hig’s companion thinks of Hig as a bit of a pussy and unusually I found myself agreeing with a redneck. <br /><br />The first part of this novel is all about survival. And I enjoyed this aspect more. The second part wanes into a cheesy love story. Hig finds Eden and Eve. And cue the hyper flowery prose. For me the parts that worked best were Hig's relationship with the natural world and his uncomfortable but life-preserving partnership with Bangley and his arsenal of weaponry. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17475251112387599176" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_431983665" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/431983665" />
    <a title="Maciek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek"><img alt="Maciek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280839159p2/2969647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/431983665?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maciek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maciek" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek">Maciek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer431983665" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15484625395406819951"><i>The Dog Stars</i> is Peter Heller's debut novel, which was promoted on Amazon as their book of the month in August of 2012, and it gathered rave reviews from the critics and readers alike. That's exactly what got me into reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13538873.Mr__Penumbra_s_24_Hour_Bookstore' title='Mr. Penumbra's 24-Hour Bookstore by Robin Sloan' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mr. Penumbra's 24-Hour Bookstore</a> - <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/505968296' rel='nofollow noopener'>which I didn't think was all that good</a> - but this one appealed to my tastes: I enjoy post-apocalyptic fiction, and thought that it could be a new and interesting addition to the genre which is now mostly populated by novels for young adults</span>
  <span id="freeText15484625395406819951" style="display:none"><i>The Dog Stars</i> is Peter Heller's debut novel, which was promoted on Amazon as their book of the month in August of 2012, and it gathered rave reviews from the critics and readers alike. That's exactly what got me into reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13538873.Mr__Penumbra_s_24_Hour_Bookstore" title="Mr. Penumbra's 24-Hour Bookstore by Robin Sloan" rel="nofollow noopener">Mr. Penumbra's 24-Hour Bookstore</a> - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/505968296" rel="nofollow noopener">which I didn't think was all that good</a> - but this one appealed to my tastes: I enjoy post-apocalyptic fiction, and thought that it could be a new and interesting addition to the genre which is now mostly populated by novels for young adults. Since it was described as "Poetic, thoughtful, transformative" and "a rare combination of the literary and highly readable", I took the bait and dove right in.<br /><br />The story takes place 9 years after an epidemic of super-flu wiped out 99,7% of the population. It's narrated by Hig, who lives in Colorado with his dog Jasper and a fellow survivor, Bangley. Mostly, Hig patrols the area in his old Cessna to look for possible hostile intruders (why there are always hostile intruders? can't people just cooperate to ensure their survival?); in his free time he makes up names of constellations (hence the title) and tries to appease Bangley, who is prone to violence and whom Hig doesn't completely trust. <br /><br />The novel is narrated by Hig, and mostly written in stacatto sentences and parapgraphs, without quotation marks for dialogue. Such broken sentence structure serves to reflect Hig's thought process, as a sentence will often end in the middle without completion, and another will begin...or not; I found it neither original nor poetic, and felt that the writing tried to hide the lack of originality of the novel; there's little here that has not already been done elsewhere, and better. It's essentially dystopian fiction 101, with all the familiar tropes. I did not find the characters and their interactions interesting, and the events leading to the ending were organized a bit too neatly for my taste. The ending itself I thought was inconsequential and out of the left field; it certainly did not cement the novel in my memory.<br /><br /><i>The Dog Stars</i> is the author's debut, and reads like a short story or a novella stretched into a novel; while there are parts of it which I enjoyed I doubt I'll think of it in a year or two, and doubt that it'll enter the dystopian canon. The first novel I've read this year was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19486.In_the_Country_of_Last_Things_" title="In the Country of Last Things  by Paul Auster" rel="nofollow noopener">In the Country of Last Things</a> by Paul Auster. Despite being shorter, Auster's work packs a whole lot more detail and punch than Heller's debut; I would recommend it to all curious readers, and advise to skip this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15484625395406819951" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_361046321" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/361046321" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2228181-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507660688p2/2228181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/361046321?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/2228181-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780449013113" href="/book/show/13330553-the-dog-stars">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2228181-jill?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer361046321" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer162279642080496145">Ask me what books made the biggest impression on me in my childhood and one of my answers would be Nevil Shute’s On The Beach – an unforgettable vision of a post-apocalyptic world. In that book, Captain Towers hears a faint Morse code signal, transmitting from far away, and heads off on a tour of a ruined world, seeking life.<br/><br/>I suspect that Peter Heller is also familiar with On The Beach. His debut book, The Dog Stars, is simply masterful. Unlike other writers of this genre, who focus on the scie</span>
  <span id="freeText162279642080496145" style="display:none">Ask me what books made the biggest impression on me in my childhood and one of my answers would be Nevil Shute’s On The Beach – an unforgettable vision of a post-apocalyptic world. In that book, Captain Towers hears a faint Morse code signal, transmitting from far away, and heads off on a tour of a ruined world, seeking life.<br /><br />I suspect that Peter Heller is also familiar with On The Beach. His debut book, The Dog Stars, is simply masterful. Unlike other writers of this genre, who focus on the scientific or other-worldly reasons for the apocalyptic event, or move forward in a plot-driven manner, Mr. Heller writes beautifully and powerfully about how the events affect one person, a contractor, poet and pilot named Hig.<br /><br />It is Hig’s fate to watch the extinction of everything and everybody he once loved: his pregnant wife Melissa, the tigers, elephants, apes, baboons and cheetahs and eventually, the last trout – a heartache for this avid fisherman.<br /><br />The prose is fragmented, accurately mimicking how someone who has not needed to talk or communicate for many years and has “lost the touch.” Hig’s only companion is a gun nut named Bangley, who is fleshed out and given an affecting back story, and his loyal and aging dog Jasper. Hig reflects, “There is no one to tell this to and yet it seems very important to get this right. The reality and what it is like to escape it. That even now it is sometimes too beautiful to bear.” And a little later, “So I wonder what it is this need to tell. To animate somehow the deathly stillness of the profoundest beauty. Breathe life in the telling.”<br /><br />That is precisely what he does. Hig will eventually try to track down a static-broken voice he heard on the radio years ago in much the same way that Captain Towers follows the enigmatic Morse code signal. And like Captain Towers, he will find comfort in surprising places.<br /><br />Much of this book is surprisingly heartbreaking in its beauty. As Hig faces the toughest existential questions about life – and wonders why he is struggling so hard to survive in a world devoid of what and who he loves the most – he learns that hope and love and decency and humanity can come when you least expect it. And he discovers that life and death live inside each other “waiting for warmer nights, a compromised system, a beetle, as in the now dying black timber on the mountain.” Mr. Heller tackles nothing less than the reasons for living and the hopeless cycles that we all must go through. This is a book that showcases an author who already possesses a voice to follow.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="162279642080496145" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_402262761" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/402262761" />
    <a title="Tim Karasko" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11598439-tim-karasko"><img alt="Tim Karasko" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1344213344p2/11598439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/402262761?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim Karasko" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim Karasko" href="/user/show/11598439-tim-karasko">Tim Karasko</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780449013113" href="/book/show/13330553-the-dog-stars">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer402262761" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9462284375694168239">In the sub-genre of post-apocalyptic fiction the reader is left with a narrative mostly bleak, bare-boned, and animalistic; "happy endings" or "humanity" are typically cast aside for a much more gritty tale of survival, with the concept of "survival" used liberally as it seems more just dumb luck that the protagonist makes it to the end of his or her ordeal. In recent years Cormac McCarthy's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road' title='The Road by Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Road</a> has been hailed as the best example of this form of fiction while in the past Neville Shute's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38180.On_the_Beach' title='On the Beach by Nevil Shute' rel='nofollow noopener'>On</a></span>
  <span id="freeText9462284375694168239" style="display:none">In the sub-genre of post-apocalyptic fiction the reader is left with a narrative mostly bleak, bare-boned, and animalistic; "happy endings" or "humanity" are typically cast aside for a much more gritty tale of survival, with the concept of "survival" used liberally as it seems more just dumb luck that the protagonist makes it to the end of his or her ordeal. In recent years Cormac McCarthy's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road" title="The Road by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Road</a> has been hailed as the best example of this form of fiction while in the past Neville Shute's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38180.On_the_Beach" title="On the Beach by Nevil Shute" rel="nofollow noopener">On the Beach</a> was a previous contender. McCarthy's vison is of ultimate bleakness, ravaged existence, and hollow individuals stripped of humanity and left with desicated emotional ties to each other; whereas Shute's end of the world was filled with so much humanity it can be argued that it partially fails due to over sentimentality. Peter Heller's "The Dog Stars" falls somewhere in between the two previous titles.<br /><br /> Heller describes a world ruined by a devastating flu mutation and also struggling with the effects of global environmental collapse. Inside this future dystopian vision a meager number of individuals struggle to make the best of what is left. The mentality of the survivors is, of course, kill or be killed with little regard for any greater future for humanity. The main protagonist, Hig, seems to be the only one left with any sense of honor or some noble sense of human kindness. Hig spends most of his time defending his home, an airfield where he keeps his 1956 Cessna operating, with the help of his ally Bangley. Bangley is stereotypical survivalist of this brand of fiction; if it moves, shoot it, if that doesn't kill it, use a bigger gun. He serves his purpose as being the darker side of Hig; a Hig that would have lost his humanity completely. This is not to say that Hig is completely sentimental about the world he lives in because he can and does kill when he is left with no choice, but he struggles with the decision. Hig eventually goes chasing his greater hope for humanity, after a moment of great loss, in the form of a radio transmission he received three years previous while flying his plane. What Hig eventually finds in this "hero's journey" is what solidifies him in his world view.<br /><br /> While the overall story is compelling to read and at times even enjoyable it is not without some flaws. The most confusing aspect of Heller's narrative is his troubling use of disjointed grammar and syntax. Heller presents the story from Hig's POV, but never completely embraces a stream of conscious form of diction; he settles for some amalgamation of SoC and traditional third-person writing styles. Heller has also completely done away with the use of quotation marks to the point of disadvantage during conversation with multiple characters and when combined with inner thoughts. The text is written in short paragraphs, or even single lines, that are separated with a single space in between that adds nothing to the narrative and seems more a vain attempt to draw attention to specific moments; it reminded me of the habit of capitalizing each letter of a word in religious tracts to "help" the reader know that something is important. The almost haphazard use of the period to chop up sentences into smaller non-sentences is almost forgivable until you come to find out that Hig is or was a published author and wrote articles for various outdoor magazines. While we have to take Hig for his word about his past he does present the reader with a generally large vocabulary and some instances of appreciation for poetry in the form of memorized lines. The discord presented by his apparent education and "writing style" due to POV doesn't seem to lend itself to improve the work of fiction overall and becomes a needless form of failed artistic flair.<br /><br /> While not as detrimental to the story as the grammar and syntax its use of pop cultural references seems out of place. Hig states that his 1956 Cessna is eighty years old which roughly puts that date of the story in the year 2036 with nine years having passed since the population collapse. Even if cultural fads stopped abruptly in 2025 Hig's memories seem to revolve around the reader's present with a reference to James Cameron's 2009 movie Avatar and an obscure reference to the song "Feed Jake" by the Pirates of the Mississippi from 1991. The only future references the reader is presented with is a possible discovery of Amelia Earhart's remains and a cure or preventative measure in regards to HIV. It becomes unbelievable that Hig would only reference such past pop cultural references as he would have been alive during countless later ones. It seems a pointless use of "real life" instances to engage the reader whom is already engaged by the story if they are continuing to read it; the references just feel out of place and break the illusion of the narrative.<br /><br /> Characters can come off as being a little underdeveloped or inconsistent with no real explanation as to why. We are never exposed to the actual first meeting between Hig and Bangley other than being told that Hig moved into the airfield and Bangley arrived soon after with his truck full of guns. Bangley, as stated before, has a "shoot first" mentality that doesn't spare men, women or children. Why did Bangley not just shoot Hig when he first saw him then at the airfield; why did he come to an understanding with Hig in the first place and not just shoot him dead? Bangley never wants to talk to anyone to see if they could be an ally or not so their partnership seems a little disingenuous to the overall characterization. This characterization flaw is as troubling as the pop cultural references so they are really only a niggling issue to an extra-aware reader. <br /><br /> The Dog Stars, in the end, is a flawed but decent first novel. It is flawed in the same ways humanity is flawed in a post-apocalyptic world; the reader must search for something to find hope in and the characters at least engage you enough to allow you be concerned about their well-being. It would have been interesting to see how this book would have turned out if it was a later work of fiction by Heller when he would be more confidant in his writing style and maybe a little less clumsy with the text. I would still recommend this to those who like post-apocalyptic narratives because it does add something to the canon of this sub-genre, good or bad it warrants a read if it even slightly interests you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9462284375694168239" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2257587661" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2257587661" />
    <a title="Antigone" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22009196-antigone"><img alt="Antigone" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1383249021p2/22009196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2257587661?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antigone" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antigone" href="/user/show/22009196-antigone">Antigone</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307950475" href="/book/show/16041830-the-dog-stars">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22009196-antigone?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2257587661" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7578435930661432805"><i>In the beginning there was Fear. Not so much the flu by then, by then I walked. I talked. Not so much talked, but of sound body - and of mind, you be the judge. Two straight weeks of fever, three days 104 to 105. I know it cooked my brains...<br/><br/>I don't want to be confused: we are nine years out. The flu killed almost everybody, then the blood disease killed more. The ones who are left are mostly Not Nice, why we live here on the plain, why I patrol every day.</i><br/><br/>His name is Hig and he settles to the te</span>
  <span id="freeText7578435930661432805" style="display:none"><i>In the beginning there was Fear. Not so much the flu by then, by then I walked. I talked. Not so much talked, but of sound body - and of mind, you be the judge. Two straight weeks of fever, three days 104 to 105. I know it cooked my brains...<br /><br />I don't want to be confused: we are nine years out. The flu killed almost everybody, then the blood disease killed more. The ones who are left are mostly Not Nice, why we live here on the plain, why I patrol every day.</i><br /><br />His name is Hig and he settles to the telling appropriately, like a hound circling and circling the rug before his bones drop to rest. His tale is a sketchy one. Also appropriate. Because when a particularly virulent pandemic hits, dispatching nine out of the ten it infects, all those people who used to provide you with information, well, they're not around anymore. You're never going to know exactly what happened or what, if anything, is being done about it now. That civil infrastructure's toast.<br /><br />Hig is living on the High Plains; that Rocky Mountain region where Nature, even in the best of circumstances, will kill you on a lark, the very instant she senses a drift in your attention. Experience provides him with the requisite awareness for survival, and the skills to maintain. Hunting, fishing, farming. He's set up residence out by the rural airport where he takes his 1956 Cessna 182 into the clouds each day to scout his perimeter. What he does <i>not</i> have, and cannot manage, is the killer instinct necessary to deal with those Not Nice intruders intent on appropriating the haven he's labored to create. That's where Bangley comes in - a merciless warrior who showed up one day in a truck filled with guns. Theirs is a prickly co-habitation, yet it serves to produce a string of tomorrows and is respected for that.<br /><br />Heller's dystopian tale is honest and intelligent. He doesn't smack you over the head with his concerns. They are, instead, insinuated organically throughout the body of his work. This subtle thread of extrapolation extends to what has true meaning and what doesn't. What we take for granted, and how very much we opt to wait upon. The way grief dawns over time - rising from denial, paralysis, vigilance - and wakens the spirit to life again. There are also, it must be said, some impressively solid action scenes that pop up every so often.<br /><br />A thoughtful book I'd recommend to those who like their stories to make sense.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7578435930661432805" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2207642797" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2207642797" />
    <a title="Rachel Bea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2805400-rachel-bea"><img alt="Rachel Bea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526942660p2/2805400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2207642797?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel Bea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel Bea" href="/user/show/2805400-rachel-bea">Rachel Bea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307950475" href="/book/show/16041830-the-dog-stars">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2805400-rachel-bea?shelf=books-of-2017">books-of-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2207642797" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5196954643096323017">For one of my reading challenges I had the option to read a book with a plane on the cover. I put a bunch of books on hold at the library and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16041830.The_Dog_Stars' title='The Dog Stars by Peter Heller' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Dog Stars</a> came in first. I've been on a genre kick lately so I was a little concerned about diving back into literary fiction, especially one that I randomly picked out because it happened to have a drawing of an airplane on its cover.<br/><br/>After reading this? I feel like it was fate for me to read.<br/><br/>The Dog Stars has now become one of my favorite books of all</span>
  <span id="freeText5196954643096323017" style="display:none">For one of my reading challenges I had the option to read a book with a plane on the cover. I put a bunch of books on hold at the library and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16041830.The_Dog_Stars" title="The Dog Stars by Peter Heller" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dog Stars</a> came in first. I've been on a genre kick lately so I was a little concerned about diving back into literary fiction, especially one that I randomly picked out because it happened to have a drawing of an airplane on its cover.<br /><br />After reading this? I feel like it was fate for me to read.<br /><br />The Dog Stars has now become one of my favorite books of all time. The writing style resonated with me right away, so from the first few words I was hooked. I would understand completely if the narrative style drove other readers nuts. But for me, it brought an intimacy to the main character - Hig - and his coping with loss and survival. <br /><br />I'm a crier. ESPECIALLY when it comes to books or movies that feature dogs. So this book? It destroyed me. (In a good way. A catharsis.) I'll never get over the death of my own dog. The friendship between Hig and Jasper, his loyal companion, reminded me the one I had and the one I'll cherish until the end of time. Reading this book stirred up the memories, the good and the bad, that comes with loving someone. Whether that someone be your pet or your partner. But this book wasn't just about loss. It was about love and hope. Family and survival. A story of surviving in a world after disease has killed 99% of the population, and learning to move on. The Dog Stars had moments of beauty and moments of tension. Where I was struck by the lyrical writing of a blossoming romance, and then a few pages later nervously turning the pages because of sudden dread. <br /><br />So for me, this book had it all. Unbelievable that until a few days ago, I had never even heard of it...</span>
  <a data-text-id="5196954643096323017" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('rc2Nm342dJCEI6JMoh1rxk8JcKP/UdBz5a+1naz9TlwdeMjV47ZbTmqu7NjZ+neQtasArWXdKp2Y+XXNso16fA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lRL8q1rg7sOr7eg40gsJ8tpFVbUCm9zUgUX2uaqocTYlp7nlx2DBHUVgpqyp7BWkIOclu5gXJjr8EzbptNhFFg==')})
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer7856874727919020237"><i>There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br/><br/><br/>Peter Heller holds an MFA from the Iowa Writers’ Workshop in both fiction and poetry. An award-winning adventure writer and longtime contributor to NPR, Heller is a contributing editor at Outside magazine, Men’s Journal, and National Geographic Adventure, and a regular contributor to Bloomberg Businessweek. He is also the autho</span>
  <span id="freeText7856874727919020237" style="display:none"><i>There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br /><br /><br />Peter Heller holds an MFA from the Iowa Writers’ Workshop in both fiction and poetry. An award-winning adventure writer and longtime contributor to NPR, Heller is a contributing editor at Outside magazine, Men’s Journal, and National Geographic Adventure, and a regular contributor to Bloomberg Businessweek. He is also the author of several nonfiction books, including Kook, The Whale Warriors, and Hell or High Water: Surviving Tibet’s Tsangpo River. He lives in Denver, Colorado.</span>
